  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }
    
    function initAppleId() {
      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',
        redirectURI: 'https://www.goodreads.com/apple_users/sign_in_with_apple_web',
        state: 'apple_oauth_state_ca11fab3-f303-4034-9dd8-5bd3ecb9dc5c'
      });
    }

      var APPLE_SIGN_IN_JS_URL =  "https://appleid.cdn-apple.com/appleauth/static/jsapi/appleid/1/en_US/appleid.auth.js"

The novel recounts the hilarious but tragic life of Murphy in London as he attempts to establish a home and to amass sufficient fortune for his intended bride to join </span>
  <span id="freeText11229167410318746780" style="display:none">'Murphy', Samuel Beckett's first published novel, was written in English and published in London in 1938; Beckett himself subsequently translated the book into French, and it was published in France in 1947. The novel recounts the hilarious but tragic life of Murphy in London as he attempts to establish a home and to amass sufficient fortune for his intended bride to join him.</span>

    <a title="Vit Babenco" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/19283284-vit-babenco"><img alt="Vit Babenco" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1365604577p2/19283284.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1239938673?book_show_action=true">Mar 29, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Vit Babenco" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Vit Babenco" href="/user/show/19283284-vit-babenco">Vit Babenco</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1239938673" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15987220082060751403"><b>Samuel Becket</b> has turned me on with the very first phrase in the book:<br/><blockquote>The sun shone, having no alternative, on the nothing new.</blockquote><br/>There is no reality, only a simulacrum of reality: individuals entertain themselves with bizarre occult practices and weird intellectual discourses:<br/><blockquote>Humanity is a well with two buckets, one going down to be filled, the other coming up to be emptied.</blockquote><br/>Isn’t it a perfect metaphor of birth and death, of knowledge and ignorance, of memory and oblivion?<br/><blockquote>Murphy’s mind pictured its</blockquote></span>
  <span id="freeText15987220082060751403" style="display:none"><b>Samuel Becket</b> has turned me on with the very first phrase in the book:<br /><blockquote>The sun shone, having no alternative, on the nothing new.</blockquote><br />There is no reality, only a simulacrum of reality: individuals entertain themselves with bizarre occult practices and weird intellectual discourses:<br /><blockquote>Humanity is a well with two buckets, one going down to be filled, the other coming up to be emptied.</blockquote><br />Isn’t it a perfect metaphor of birth and death, of knowledge and ignorance, of memory and oblivion?<br /><blockquote>Murphy’s mind pictured itself as a large hollow sphere, hermetically closed to the universe without. This was not an impoverishment, for it excluded nothing that it did not itself contain. Nothing ever had been, was or would be in the universe outside it but was already present as virtual, or actual, or virtual rising into actual, or actual falling into virtual, in the universe inside it.</blockquote><br />A mind like this doesn’t need any company except its own. And it has no other way but down and out…<br />Our souls are kites on a string that dream to break free into eternity…</span>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_267552575" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/267552575" />
    <a title="Richard Derus" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3920693-richard-derus"><img alt="Richard Derus" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1494992210p2/3920693.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/267552575?book_show_action=true">Jan 25, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Richard Derus" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Richard Derus" href="/user/show/3920693-richard-derus">Richard Derus</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780714500423" href="/book/show/54033.Murphy">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer267552575" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9852265695792615081">Scintillating, superb, fractal geometry in words.<br/><br/>Yeah, that verdict stands too.<br/><br/>Rating: 5* of five<br/><br/><b>The Publisher Says</b>: <i>MURPHY</i>, when first published in 1938, was Beckett's first novel and third work of ficiton. Very Irish in the post-Joycean tradition, it nevertheless was the beginning of a new form of literary expression as some discerning critics recognized at the time, drawing heavily on the author's time spent in London as a young man, and especially on his experiences as a male nurse. It has f</span>
  <span id="freeText9852265695792615081" style="display:none">Scintillating, superb, fractal geometry in words.<br><br>Yeah, that verdict stands too.<br><br>Rating: 5* of five<br><br><b>The Publisher Says</b>: <i>MURPHY</i>, when first published in 1938, was Beckett's first novel and third work of ficiton. Very Irish in the post-Joycean tradition, it nevertheless was the beginning of a new form of literary expression as some discerning critics recognized at the time, drawing heavily on the author's time spent in London as a young man, and especially on his experiences as a male nurse. It has for many years been one of the most popular novels of one of the most seminal figures of the twentieth century. There's a sort of comfort in the dry, bare, language, in the spooky imagery and the incantatory repetitions... -- Howard Kinlay. <br><br><b>My Review</b>: This is always hard when describing Beckett. Murphy loves Celia, the prostitute. Miss Counihan, surprisingly anthropoid for an Irishwoman, loves Murphy. Neary, a philosopher, comes to love Murphy as his best friend. Then there's this guy Murphy plays chess with in the mental hospital where Murphy goes to work.<br><br>Okay, it can't really be this hard. Murphy, an Irish depressive, has to get a job because Celia, his petite amie, thinks it will do him good. So he leaves Ireland, goes to London and starts working at a mental hospital. All sorts of Irish problems follow him, but Murphy finds himself escaping them among the mad, who have abdicated their responsibilities to the staff and lead lives of unencumbered irresponsibility that Murphy envies. Mr. Endon, the wisest madman, lures Murphy into playing a game of chess with him, and it's that game that forms the spine of the book. It's described in loving, and to me incomprehensible, detail, but if you're patient and willing to educate yourself with a chess reference source as you read, you'll come to realize that this game is the novel you're reading, and the novel is the chess game.<br><br>How do chess games end? Think on that for a moment. The novel's ending will then be clear to you. It's not the easiest read on the shelf. It's well worth your time and effort to engage with, because it's gorgeously wrought...there's a line about owls in the zoo, their joys and sufferings not starting until dark, that I wish I could find so as to quote exactly, but it's...well...perfect, and at the moment it comes in the narrative, so startlingly apt that it makes my hair stand up to remember it.<br><br>Beckett hated Ireland for its conformist, dead-spirited religiosity. He abhorred any and all forms of hypocrisy, and this (I think, could be wrong about this) is the last novel he wrote in English because he regarded the language as the carrier (think Typhoid Mary) of hypocrisy. (So what did he do? He wrote in FRENCH! Oh the irony.) <i>Murphy</i> is the soul-scream of an angry lover. It caustically throws in your face every unkind or unworthy thought you've ever had, every casually cruel deed you've ever done, and makes you weep and smile and sigh with pleasure as it alternately berates and caresses you.<br><br>Yes, this book is a bad love affair with a beautiful man put between hard covers. It's brilliant, it's beautiful, and it's never to be forgotten, even when you wish you could.<br><br><a href="http://creativecommons.org/licenses/by-nc-sa/3.0/deed.en_US" rel="nofollow noopener">
  <img alt="Creative Commons License" src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1380148217i/680962.png" class="gr-hostedUserImg">
</a><br>This work is licensed under a <a href="http://creativecommons.org/licenses/by-nc-sa/3.0/deed.en_US" rel="nofollow noopener">Creative Commons Attribution-NonCommercial-ShareAlike 3.0 Unported License</a>.</span>
  <a data-text-id="9852265695792615081" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_401789951" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/401789951" />
    <a title="K.D. Absolutely" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2156200-k-d-absolutely"><img alt="K.D. Absolutely" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1471967140p2/2156200.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/401789951?book_show_action=true">Aug 27, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="K.D. Absolutely" class="user" itemprop="url" name="K.D. Absolutely" href="/user/show/2156200-k-d-absolutely">K.D. Absolutely</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to K.D. by: </span>
      1001 Books You Must Read Before You Die (2006-2010)
  </div>


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2156200-k-d-absolutely?shelf=humour">humour</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2156200-k-d-absolutely?shelf=challenging">challenging</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2156200-k-d-absolutely?shelf=hospital-drama">hospital-drama</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2156200-k-d-absolutely?shelf=modernist">modernist</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2156200-k-d-absolutely?shelf=philosophical">philosophical</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2156200-k-d-absolutely?shelf=saddest">saddest</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2156200-k-d-absolutely?shelf=post-modernist">post-modernist</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2156200-k-d-absolutely?shelf=surreal">surreal</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2156200-k-d-absolutely?shelf=tragicomic">tragicomic</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2156200-k-d-absolutely?shelf=1001-core">1001-core</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer401789951" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10135237805277647644">My very first Beckett. Yesterday morning, when I finally closed this book, I was speechless. This is a work of a genius.<br/><br/>Reading the first half was a bit of a challenge though. If you are not used to reading modernist or postmodernist works, there is a big chance that Beckett's writing style would discourage you. Samuel Beckett (1906-1989) apprenticed with his friend and idol - my other favorite Irish writer - the author of <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/338798.Ulysses' title='Ulysses by James Joyce' rel='nofollow noopener'>Ulysses</a> (5 stars <i>too!</i>), James Joyce (1882-1941). So, their styles have si</span>
  <span id="freeText10135237805277647644" style="display:none">My very first Beckett. Yesterday morning, when I finally closed this book, I was speechless. This is a work of a genius.<br /><br />Reading the first half was a bit of a challenge though. If you are not used to reading modernist or postmodernist works, there is a big chance that Beckett's writing style would discourage you. Samuel Beckett (1906-1989) apprenticed with his friend and idol - my other favorite Irish writer - the author of <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/338798.Ulysses" title="Ulysses by James Joyce" rel="nofollow noopener">Ulysses</a> (5 stars <i>too!</i>), James Joyce (1882-1941). So, their styles have similarities but still distinctly recognizable. I liked them both. Although, "Murphy" of course is a lot shorter and easier to understand than "Ulysses."<br /><br />But I have to admit again that I am not sure I understood everything that Beckett wanted to convey in this book. Like the attitude that I used for me to finish "Ulysses," I just went on and on with my reading even if I had to reread some paragraphs twice or thrice. This explains why it took me 1 month to finish this book. There were so many parts where I asked myself: <i>"Huh? Again? What did he say?"</i> However, in the second half, when <b>Murphy</b> was already working as a nurse in the hospital for crazy people, that's when the story made a huge turn and everything started to fall into right places. The conversation of his friends at the sickbed of Murphy's girlfriend <b>Celia</b> was the first scene that left me in awe because the prose, the playful beautiful prose, was something that I have not encountered yet even in his friend James Joyce's works. Then that scene was closely followed by the well-known chess game that <i>seems</i> to have encapsulated or mimicked or mirrored the whole novel. I am not good in playing chess and I don't exactly know how to read the written moves and I saw this style in <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/83346.Through_the_Looking_Glass__and_What_Alice_Found_There" title="Through the Looking-Glass, and What Alice Found There by Lewis Carroll" rel="nofollow noopener">Through the Looking-Glass, and What Alice Found There</a> (3 stars) by Lewis Carroll but the listed explanations (letters <i>a</i> to <i>r</i>) at the bottom of the game sequence adequently explained to a non-chess playing person like me how the game relates to the characters in the novel. Brilliant. Brilliant. Brilliant.<br /><br />Beckett's play of words is something that enthralled me. Even the effective use of font like: Music, Music, Music with the font going from 10pts to 14pts seems like meaningful or his repeated use of a phrase like "A rest" then a paragraph, the another "A rest", then another paragraph, and then another "A rest." Then you as a reader will ask yourself silent in your mind: <i>Why? What's happening?"</i> then you will later realize. You can't help but be mesmerized by his skillful execution in writing. I have not seen anything as effective as Beckett's style.<br /><br />My advice to those who want to read this: don't be too bothered about the specifics in the first half. Just continue reading because the meat is in the second half where everything will become crystal clear. <br /><br />Guaranteed. To. Take. Your. Breath. Away!</span>
  <a data-text-id="10135237805277647644" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_117910834" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/117910834" />
    <a title="Ellen" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2927182-ellen"><img alt="Ellen" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1500520747p2/2927182.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/117910834?book_show_action=true">Aug 21, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ellen" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ellen" href="/user/show/2927182-ellen">Ellen</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780394172101" href="/book/show/1201185.Murphy">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2927182-ellen?shelf=novel">novel</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2927182-ellen?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer117910834" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer991305370096183248">Thought I'd review this as so few have, and this is Beckett's first, best, and funniest novel.<p></p><br/>Many people think of Beckett's characters, and they come up with something like this:<br/><p></p><br/><img src='https://s3.amazonaws.com/photo.goodreads.com/photos/1282410933p8/145732.jpg' width='300' height='220' alt='description' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/><br/><p></p><br/>These post-apocalyptic, hollowed-out characters sitting around in the blank landscapes of Beckett's plays have been blasted to hell and back; they patiently accept whatever happens to them and seem to be waiting for some form of grace.<br/><br/>In contrast, Murphy (the title character of the novel) isn't going anywhere, but he co</span>
  <span id="freeText991305370096183248" style="display:none">Thought I'd review this as so few have, and this is Beckett's first, best, and funniest novel.<p></p><br>Many people think of Beckett's characters, and they come up with something like this:<br><p></p><br><img src="https://s3.amazonaws.com/photo.goodreads.com/photos/1282410933p8/145732.jpg" width="300" height="220" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><p></p><br>These post-apocalyptic, hollowed-out characters sitting around in the blank landscapes of Beckett's plays have been blasted to hell and back; they patiently accept whatever happens to them and seem to be waiting for some form of grace.<br><br>In contrast, Murphy (the title character of the novel) isn't going anywhere, but he couldn't be happier. Unlike so many of the characters in Beckett's plays, Murphy is bursting with life. He might be one hell of an odd duck, but he's alive and hopping. Murphy has no desire to be housebroken by society or to parcel out his days and nights as productively as a character like Robinson Crusoe. Murphy isn't alarmed by the void that sends Lucy Snowe (<i>Villette</i>) into a near frenzy, but rather seeks out these unstructured spaces actively. <br><br>To achieve personal bliss, Murphy sits tied--by seven scarves--and naked "in his rocking chair of undressed teak, guaranteed not to crack, warp, shrink, corrode, or creak at night." This rather bizarre arrangement works to give his body pleasure and "free his mind": "For it was not until his body was appeased that he could come alive in his mind....And life in his mind gave him pleasure, such pleasure that pleasure was not the word."<br><br>In short, Murphy isn't heading anywhere in a hurry; his lack of what we might view as motivation parodies time, a point one could gather from the book's opening line, which neatly lampoons all the novels with predictably dull first lines about weather and time, and also lets us know we aren't in Kansas anymore: <blockquote>The sun shone, having no alternative, on the nothing new.</blockquote><br><br>I've been thinking a lot about humor this summer because I'm pondering whether to teach a course on the topic. While I think <i>Murphy</i> is one of the funniest books in literature, it's hard to define why. Like explaining a joke, which falls apart upon examination, nothing destroys humor faster than theorizing its characteristics. And it's pretty telling that those who have tried to define humor--Aristotle, Sigmund Freud, Henri Bergson, and Immanuel Kant, to name a few--are some pretty humorless dudes. Perhaps funny people know better than to try to define funny-dom. <br><br>Prodded by Celia, his good-hearted but mercenary prostitute girlfriend, Murphy reluctantly sets out to find employment: "Murphy on the jobpath was a striking figure..." Beckett goes on at length about the qualities of the green suit Murphy sports: <blockquote>"His suit was not green but aeruginous....No less than the colour the cut was striking. The jacket, a tube in its own right, descended clear of the body as far as mid-thigh, where the skirts were slightly reflexed like the mouth of a bell in mute appeal to be lifted that some found hard to resist....With regard to the material of the suit, the bold claim was advanced by the makers that it was holeproof. This was true in the sense that it was entirely non-porous. It admitted no air from the outer world, it allowed none of Murphy's own vapours to escape.</blockquote><br>Murphy's unusual aspect is noted by some of his potential employers. He's turned down for the job of "smart boy" at Gray's Inn Road, where he's assessed harshly:"'E ain't smart...."Nor 'e ain't a boy" [and:]"'E don't look rightly human to me." But luck shines on Murphy and he gets a job as a male nurse at Magdalen Mental Mercyseat, "a hospital for the better-class mentally deranged." Unaware that Murphy is happily employed by M.M.M., most of the book's characters are busily searching for him.<br><br>Despite the rather elaborate plot--which mocks plots in general--the book is not about the story arc. Whatever absurd situations confront Murphy are ultimately beside the point. The book's focus is on Murphy and his desire to avoid the sound and fury, all the tomorrows and tomorrows and tomorrows that natter at us and make us conform to a world that fits about as well as Murphy's green suit. As Murphy explains, "I am not of the big world, I am of the little world."<br><br>* * *<br> <br>And, as a added boon, some of Beckett's better phrases:<br><blockquote>"mercantile gehenna" - the work world<br>"long hank of Apollonian asthenia," "schizoidal spasmophile," "seedy solipsist" - words to call your friends<br>"jacking their jaws apart with the gag, spurning their tongues aside with the spatula, till the last tundish of drench is absorbed" - Ticklepenny describing how he feeds the patients at Magdalene Mental Mercyseat</blockquote><br><br><br></span>
  <a data-text-id="991305370096183248" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_3342938355" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3342938355" />
    <a title="MJ Nicholls" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2386804-mj-nicholls"><img alt="MJ Nicholls" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1374341850p2/2386804.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3342938355?book_show_action=true">May 20, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="MJ Nicholls" class="user" itemprop="url" name="MJ Nicholls" href="/user/show/2386804-mj-nicholls">MJ Nicholls</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780714500423" href="/book/show/54033.Murphy">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2386804-mj-nicholls?shelf=novels">novels</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2386804-mj-nicholls?shelf=pogue-mahone">pogue-mahone</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3342938355" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16584625228572032037">Having started and stopped this novel for years and yonks, professing a love for Beckett’s trilogy and later prose works in the interim, I was more disposed to appreciating this first novel at the nineteenth attempt of reading. To my annoyance, the lights were not switched on to the same alleluia choir as with <i>Molloy</i> or <i>Malone Dies</i>—the wit, the absurd contortions of prose logic, the frequent deployment of recondite words and terms, the same grimly hilarious worldview of the plays—all this was re</span>
  <span id="freeText16584625228572032037" style="display:none">Having started and stopped this novel for years and yonks, professing a love for Beckett’s trilogy and later prose works in the interim, I was more disposed to appreciating this first novel at the nineteenth attempt of reading. To my annoyance, the lights were not switched on to the same alleluia choir as with <i>Molloy</i> or <i>Malone Dies</i>—the wit, the absurd contortions of prose logic, the frequent deployment of recondite words and terms, the same grimly hilarious worldview of the plays—all this was registered. Lacking was the sublime music of later Beckett, the symphonies of blissful density present in the trilogy. I found a mashup of the wilful obscurity of the early stories, the first flourishings of Beckettian dialogue, and the earliest rumblings of the later prose rhythms, not yet distilled into something over which I might rant and rave. In other words, make mine a Molloy or a Malone, you can keep your Murphy. </span>
  <a data-text-id="16584625228572032037" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">

              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6249204-antonomasia?shelf=2015">2015</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6249204-antonomasia?shelf=j">j</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6249204-antonomasia?shelf=ireland">ireland</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6249204-antonomasia?shelf=1001-books">1001-books</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6249204-antonomasia?shelf=decade-1930s">decade-1930s</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer261407770" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13642714536590827906">[4.5] Nine years ago, I was in love with someone whose favourite book was <i>Murphy</i>; this copy was from a second hand bookshop en route between our flats. (He was one of those people who never lends anything, and in any case I wouldn't have been able to read and return it in good time, as this time that year I was embarking on months of convalescence following a very severe bout of flu.) It was never my intention to leave it quite this long, but - as per an old <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/250544436?book_show_action=false&page=1#comment_58521519' rel='nofollow noopener'>comment</a> - whenever I picked it up, I </span>
  <span id="freeText13642714536590827906" style="display:none">[4.5] Nine years ago, I was in love with someone whose favourite book was <i>Murphy</i>; this copy was from a second hand bookshop en route between our flats. (He was one of those people who never lends anything, and in any case I wouldn't have been able to read and return it in good time, as this time that year I was embarking on months of convalescence following a very severe bout of flu.) It was never my intention to leave it quite this long, but - as per an old <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/250544436?book_show_action=false&amp;page=1#comment_58521519" rel="nofollow noopener">comment</a> - whenever I picked it up, I found the first couple of pages too depressing. <br /><br />Honestly, what was I reading? Must've made some skewed assumptions, for different stuff was happening on the paper this time. And Beckett is not the writer I thought he was, a minimalist miserablist - my only previous experience being a not-very-careful teenage reading of <i>Waiting For Godot</i> whilst standing in a library, it being too short to waste a loan on. I was reminded of none so much as Pynchon: the black humour, the ridiculousness, the near-farcical caper, the breadth of vocabulary... this required even more frequent looking-up than Pynchon as Beckett's very fond of obscure uses of fairly common words, as well as his rare archaisms and classicisms (Joycean to those who've read <i>him</i> more recently, no doubt). A spideysense of more to a sentence often proved right: consulting the barely adequate ereader dictionary, and looking through definitions, would reveal an unusual or more precise meaning, or perhaps that an etymological root had been engaged in furtive wordplay with something else nearby. Impressed, I was alright. (Why can we still not get the <i>Shorter Oxford</i>, never mind the full thing, as an optional booster for these gadgets? c.35% of the terms I tried to look up whilst reading <i>Murphy</i> were not in the Concise as found on Kindle.) <br /> <br />Why (2) isn't <i>Murphy</i> found on some of those lists of Great London Novels? Not that I've done a comprehensive survey as research for this post, mind. Murphy's questionable progression from the 1930s boardinghouses of SW10 and then - with paramour Celia - N7, and on to work in one of those outer-North-London psychiatric monstrosities as later chronicled by Iain Sinclair and Will Self ... not to mention the wanderings of so many named streets and tubes and markets, and the Mary Poppins-in-negative interludes of kite flying in Hyde Park - this is what The London Novel is made of, surely. There are chapters set in Ireland too, which in the eyes of some may disqualify it, but <i>Murphy</i>'s atmosphere of grubby, fray-cuffed, straitened, eccentric London bedsitland is exquisitely done if you have affection for that setting. Description of the way light comes through the windows of a dingy flat over a day's course may not be adjectivally conventional <i>description</i>, yet - or so - it pins it utterly.<br /><br />I long had a bad habit, a faulty reflex, almost, of looking for a person in their favourite books and films (more so when it was someone I fancied, a elusive sprite, than a long-standing close friend who could simply be asked). This wouldn't always work, I realised as I got older. Not everyone's favourite things are favourites because of a deep identification. And even where someone does identify, it won't be with every aspect of a work or character - so a person who doesn't know them well could misunderstand and missassume all over the shop. Perhaps you actually won't see how <i>them</i> something is until you've known them a good while. (Seven years it was, mostly the long-distance friendship of the afterwards, and it was me who stopped it - there was a veiled reference in my post about <i>The Ice Palace</i>.) And I can't remember when this strange wish of finding someone in a book was so fully rewarded; a remarkable correlation of traits between unusual people, one real, one made up by a very clever Irish bloke in the 1930s. It would be indecent to write it all up in public, but I consider it incumbent on me to spare the (theoretical??) embarrassment of someone unknown to you by clarifying that the similarities to Murphy <i>don't</i> include penchants for self-bondage or astrology. Curiously or not, there are a handful of other things that feel more *me* than *him*; some always were, some changed - and as for those shared, I now understand Murphy's love of introspection more than I would have nearly a decade ago. <br /><br />The character of Murphy is, of course, ultimately mocked here (his Cartesian dualism is as much nonsense as that theory always is) and the book's peculiar personal history may give me more affection for it and all its characters than might otherwise make sense. (Given its absurdist intellectualism, I can understand those who don't find it very human.)<br /><br />This is very close to 5 stars - just some scenes of grimness mean I hold back on that last little bit - as the confluence of extreme cleverness and silliness and darkness and resonance is very much a winner. And if I didn't have an even more absurd number of other books already, I'd be wanting some more Beckett, please, with a tentative view to joining Fans of. </span>
  <a data-text-id="13642714536590827906" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2296101781" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4829028985001645460"><i>Murphy</i> is a strange, sad, melancholic and sometimes funny novel on madness, boredom, misunderstanding and isolation. Murphy, our main character and isolated 'solipsist', stuck in a void, jobless and work-shy, decides to withdraw from life away from his prostitute fiance. What follows is Beckett's bleak and absurd humour on everyday life, an outlook shared by his alter ego on paper, Murphy, and his ambitious wordplay. When writers treat language as if it were gold, that's when a work of literatur</span>
  <span id="freeText4829028985001645460" style="display:none"><i>Murphy</i> is a strange, sad, melancholic and sometimes funny novel on madness, boredom, misunderstanding and isolation. Murphy, our main character and isolated 'solipsist', stuck in a void, jobless and work-shy, decides to withdraw from life away from his prostitute fiance. What follows is Beckett's bleak and absurd humour on everyday life, an outlook shared by his alter ego on paper, Murphy, and his ambitious wordplay. When writers treat language as if it were gold, that's when a work of literature becomes something special. <i>Murphy</i> is certainly one of those. Now, where's the Trilogy...?</span>

<div id="review_13316347" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/13316347" />
    <a title="Andrew" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/123397-andrew"><img alt="Andrew" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1536347998p2/123397.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/13316347?book_show_action=true">Jan 23, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Andrew" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Andrew" href="/user/show/123397-andrew">Andrew</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer13316347" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5015438080808714534">I've read this one twice, and am gearing up for another it's such a warm hug. I was going back through it recently looking for something and found a list I made of words looked up while reading it. Here it is, in case anyone else would find this helpful. <br/>Beckett’s Murphy words (italics notes from 4 years later) <br/><br/>‘rutting cur’s rejectementa’: <br/>rutting - furrow, groove, a fixed or established mode of procedure or course of life, usually dull or unpromising<br/>cur – worthless coward, <br/>rejectamenta – worth</span>
  <span id="freeText5015438080808714534" style="display:none">I've read this one twice, and am gearing up for another it's such a warm hug. I was going back through it recently looking for something and found a list I made of words looked up while reading it. Here it is, in case anyone else would find this helpful. <br />Beckett’s Murphy words (italics notes from 4 years later) <br /><br />‘rutting cur’s rejectementa’: <br />rutting - furrow, groove, a fixed or established mode of procedure or course of life, usually dull or unpromising<br />cur – worthless coward, <br />rejectamenta – worthless things<br />reversioner - one that has or is entitled to a reversion; broadly : someone having a vested right to a future estate <br />softsoap (verb) – to cajole, flatter (to use soft soap in washing)<br />pensum - ‘improved out’ danish or norwegian for syllabus<br />tuffets everted - A clump or tuft of grass; A low seat, such as a stool.<br />the moon at perigree - point nearest to earth in a body orbiting it<br />a fake jossy – ??<br />‘tipstaff with warrant to distain’ <br />tipstaff – an attendant or crier in a court of law; a staff tipped with metal, formerly carried as a badge of office, as by a constable.<br />distain – sully, stain, discolor<br />bumbailiff- I thought this was just a portmanteau of bumbling and bailiff but it turns out: server of writs, maker of arrests, etc., 1601, from bum "arse," because he was always felt to be close behind.<br />neap – of a tide<br />naevus – birthmark<br />suspiration – breathing, sighing<br />dun - to make repeated and insistent demands upon, esp. for the payment of a debt; a person, esp. a creditor, who duns another; a demand for payment, esp. a written one. <br />thema coeli – theme<br />whinge – whine<br />heard bilge- the widest circumference or belly of a cask.; to bulge or swell out<br />Bollitoes (should be Ballitoes)- a brand of silk stockings secured with suspenders to corset or belt<br />cuchilion – (Cuchulain alt.) A hero of ancient Ulster who single-handedly defended it against the rest of Ireland; cuchillo 2nd alt. - knife<br />the mercantile gehenna - the valley of Hinnom, near Jerusalem, where propitiatory sacrifices were made to Moloch. II Kings 23:10; any place of extreme suffering, hell<br />diastolesystole - in prosody, systole is the shortening of a syllable that is by pronunciation or by position long. Systole is most often used to adjust the rhythm of a line to achieve metrical regularity. The word is from the Greek systole, meaning, literally, “contraction.” Diastole, the opposite of systole, is the lengthening of a short quantity or syllable for metric irregularity.<br />not green but aeruginous – bluish green, copper<br />parthian – designating a glance/ remark delivered by a person departing (parting shot)<br />‘serenade, nocturne, albada’ – spanish for nocturne serenade<br />synecdoche - A figure of speech in which a part is used for the whole (as hand for sailor), the whole for a part (as the law for police officer), the specific for the general (as cutthroat for assassin), the general for the specific (as thief for pickpocket), or the material for the thing made from it (as steel for sword).<br />lee – protective shelter; region toward which the wind blows<br />cozeners, cozened – to cozen is to cheat, deceive or trick (amazing that the name of my dad’s old law firm was cozen and o’conner- “to cheat and con”- great!<br />Cony-catching – same thing as cozen- cheat deceive trick<br />tumbril - one of the carts used during the French Revolution to convey victims to the guillotine; a farmer's cart, esp. one for hauling manure, that can be tilted to discharge its load.<br />child’s halma - long jump with weight in hands (ancient greece) or some sor tof old board game<br />gambadoes, worked up<br />‘in this kip’ - the hide of a young or small beas; also a rooming house or place to sleep<br />‘the plaisir de rompre: for Murphy the rationale of social contracts’ french for putting an end to<br />triorchous – three testicled<br />in doss, dossed – place to sleep; to sleep in a convenient place<br />curate - cleric<br />‘derogating from the general to the particular’ – he’s using the archaic definition - to take away (a part) so as to impair the whole, which I still don’t understand, but the usual definition is to detract from authority<br />will not parley – a discussion; to talk<br />wantum - ??<br />from the hegira – flight to escape danger<br />amnion - A thin, tough, membranous sac that encloses the embryo<br />her ruelle – french for alley<br />clumsy genustuprations – ??, based on context I’d say some sort of sexual advance<br />lost tundish of drench - funnel<br />dipso - drunk<br />prosodoturfy – based on context and roots, I’d guess ‘native habits of speaking/ writing’<br />senechalesque figure of 5 lb.s - ??<br />induration to abuse – pathological hardening<br />syzygy - an alignment of three celestial objects, as the sun, the earth, and either the moon or a planet; any two related things, either alike or opposite<br />permangate - ??, permanganate is a chemical compound of potassium and some other shit<br />nosonomy – taxonomoy of diseases<br />condole – to express sympathy<br />spado – impotent, castrated person<br />‘touching little argonautic maieutic saws’ - pertaining to the method used by Socrates of eliciting knowledge in the mind of a person by interrogation and insistence on close and logical reasoning. <br />hypermetropic - abnormal ability to focus of distant objects <br />pellucid - clear in meaning<br />quoits - a game in which rings of rope or flattened metal are thrown at an upright peg, the object being to encircle it or come as close to it as possible.<br />irrefragable - not to be disputed or contested.<br />scarlet ampelopsis - a climbing, woody vine or shrub having small greenish flowers and inedible berries.<br />consuum - ??<br />lanugo - a coat of delicate, downy hairs, esp. that with which the human fetus or a newborn infant is covered.<br />afflatulence – affable farts<br />protasis - The dependent clause of a conditional sentence; a stretching towards<br />Lassata – seems like a random proper noun<br />canaille – riffraff; the masses<br />obole – medieval coin<br />soughing - A soft murmuring or rustling sound<br />involute – intricate, complex, curled, curved<br />abolla - a woolen cloak worn by men in ancient Rome. <br />coadjutor – assistant to an ecclesiastic type<br />clave - one of a pair of wooden sticks or blocks that are held one in each hand and are struck together to accompany music and dancing. I don’t think this is how he was using it. I think he used it as an archaic form of ‘cleave.’<br />fillip - anything that tends to rouse, excite, or revive; a stimulus<br />immured – to imprison<br />gyves – shackles for the legs<br />postmonitions – a warning after the fact<br />sheriffalty – jurisdiction of a sheriff<br />‘cyanosis of youth’ - A bluish discoloration of the skin and mucous membranes resulting from inadequate oxygenation of the blood<br />fug – stale air<br />aposiopoesis - a sudden breaking off in the midst of a sentence, as if from inability or unwillingness to proceed<br />plutolaters - ??<br />analphabet – person who can’t read or write<br />crispated - rough<br />apnoen - ??<br />preterist - ??<br />erebus - The dark region of the underworld through which the dead must pass before they reach Hades. <br />‘low battuta of anger’ – a beat, measure <br />‘vulpine endowments’ – cunning, crafty<br />acathisia - a condition characterized by uncontrollable motor restlessness<br />‘palatial tantalus’ - a stand or rack containing visible decanters, esp. of wines or liquors, secured by a lock<br />fusee - a spirally grooved spindle in a clock that counteracts the diminishing power of the uncoiling mainspring; a colored flare used as a warning signal by trucks and trains; a friction match with a large head that will stay alight in the wind; any igniter that is used to initiate the burning of a propellant <br />contumely - insulting display of contempt in words or actions<br />‘instinct was a menstrum’ - A solvent, especially one used in extracting compounds from plant and animal tissues and preparing drugs<br />eleutheromania – a mania for freedom<br />depilatories – agents capable of removing hair<br />‘vermigrade wave of light’ – worm-shaped<br />‘turn to civet’ - a yellowish, unctuous substance with a strong musklike odor, obtained from a pouch in the genital region of civets and used in perfumery.<br />niobaloo – p. 131 of “Murphy's Bed: A Study of Real Sources and Sur-Real Associations in Samuel Beckett’s First Novel” by Sighle Kennedy is the best resource if you really want to know what he’s doing with this word<br />clonic – relating to convulsion characterized by alternating contractions and relaxations <br />‘oakum of her history’ - loose fibers<br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="5015438080808714534" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<span id="freeTextContainer4855580448874073038">Only lacking in stars because I can't earnestly say I understood everything I read. I read this book over about a week and I still had to go back frequently to go look up exactly what the characters' relations to each other was, and more. I never felt dense reading this book, however, and thanks to reading on my Kindle, often times when I looked up a word's definition it felt like I was being let in on a punchline. There are some truly fantastic quotables in Murphy, some from the gorgeous langua</span>
  <span id="freeText4855580448874073038" style="display:none">Only lacking in stars because I can't earnestly say I understood everything I read. I read this book over about a week and I still had to go back frequently to go look up exactly what the characters' relations to each other was, and more. I never felt dense reading this book, however, and thanks to reading on my Kindle, often times when I looked up a word's definition it felt like I was being let in on a punchline. There are some truly fantastic quotables in Murphy, some from the gorgeous language and context of the plot, but more from Beckett's cunning wordplay and humor. <br />"It was true that Cooper never sat, his acathisia was deep-seated and of long standing..."<br />^ That is probably one of the cornier jokes, but I still found it amusing enough to highlight. <br /><br />I was mostly grateful to read this book to see that Beckett's prose is not necessarily all like his absurdist and minimal theatre works, and I am eager to continue with more Beckett novels. </span>

<div id="review_1535543397" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1535543397" />
    <a title="Cymru Roberts" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/8997708-cymru-roberts"><img alt="Cymru Roberts" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1603468446p2/8997708.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1535543397?book_show_action=true">Feb 02, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Cymru Roberts" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Cymru Roberts" href="/user/show/8997708-cymru-roberts">Cymru Roberts</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/8997708-cymru-roberts?shelf=20th-century-elsewhere">20th-century-elsewhere</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/8997708-cymru-roberts?shelf=beckett">beckett</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1535543397" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8812120308115392328">As a first effort, Beckett demonstrates that he has total control over the conventions of a novel. The tricky ways in which he unravels exposition, the exquisite wordplay (keep dictionary on hand), and not least the characters themselves all attest to his strength as a writer. He also has sensory-perception and an ear for tone, a "viridescent" tone in fact, which is lovely. Combined with deep imaginative understanding, this book is heavier than it reads. It reads like a breeze. And funny to boot</span>
  <span id="freeText8812120308115392328" style="display:none">As a first effort, Beckett demonstrates that he has total control over the conventions of a novel. The tricky ways in which he unravels exposition, the exquisite wordplay (keep dictionary on hand), and not least the characters themselves all attest to his strength as a writer. He also has sensory-perception and an ear for tone, a "viridescent" tone in fact, which is lovely. Combined with deep imaginative understanding, this book is heavier than it reads. It reads like a breeze. And funny to boot!<br /><br />First published in 1938, Murph predates Pynchon and Vonnegut (Why aren't these authors compared more often? Just cuz Pynch is "smart" I guess). As a verbal freestyler, it's hard to argue any of the later literary tricksters are as keen or poetic as Beckett. The drawback to the arch-sarcastic literary mode is that it can be hard to take anything seriously, like, when someone dies for instance. Becky is tender enough in parts here however to eschew a total lack of caring for plot, and Murph's eventual post-demise is one of my favourite scenes ever. If we all could be utilized so!<br /><br />If this is Beck's first book, I can imagine why he won the Nobel Prize. I plan on going in chronological order with him. I like doing that with authors.</span>
  <a data-text-id="8812120308115392328" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer6165992605160369418">Ten years ago I read this for the first time on an airplane and the second time five minutes later, which should show how much I loved and did not quite understand it. But then somehow after the first flush one shrinks, into a small, but quite virulent Beckett-hater. All those "jokes". All that "despair". The verbal brilliance of a martian teenager coating what is essentially the Olde Irish Romance: in love with what kicks us.   <span id="freeText6165992605160369418" style="display:none">Ten years ago I read this for the first time on an airplane and the second time five minutes later, which should show how much I loved and did not quite understand it. But then somehow after the first flush one shrinks, into a small, but quite virulent Beckett-hater. All those "jokes". All that "despair". The verbal brilliance of a martian teenager coating what is essentially the Olde Irish Romance: in love with what kicks us. And yet here are the sanitarium chapters, where B does in fact seem to leave behind the I-don't-wanna-grow-up desire of his hero. From this point on, he will be idiot-bit, meaning fascinated by minds trapped in their own rigor. An Oblomovian book (thank you V.S. Pritchett), a young man's attempt to mix French confidence and native Irish chicanery, into a literary MacFlurry of Dantean flavor, if not proportions. For all its despair, Murphy is the paradise. Thought perhaps this made it Beckett's hell...</span>
  <a data-text-id="6165992605160369418" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_1094816989" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1094816989" />
    <a title="Graham P" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3924545-graham-p"><img alt="Graham P" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1515503736p2/3924545.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1094816989?book_show_action=true">Nov 01, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Graham P" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Graham P" href="/user/show/3924545-graham-p">Graham P</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780802144454" href="/book/show/8585893-murphy">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1094816989" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer281137008352017722">A brilliant little novel that explodes majestic. One might call it a parody of manners, or a scatter-brained farce of love and desire. It's all that and much more. My first foray into Beckett, and I must admit that I wasn't deterred by his obtuse, madcap and heartbreaking observations about the struggles of day-to-day life. His prose reads like a lonely man's guide to madness where epiphanies pass like gas, and boredom develops its owns manic design. While he writes at angles (and around corners</span>
  <span id="freeText281137008352017722" style="display:none">A brilliant little novel that explodes majestic. One might call it a parody of manners, or a scatter-brained farce of love and desire. It's all that and much more. My first foray into Beckett, and I must admit that I wasn't deterred by his obtuse, madcap and heartbreaking observations about the struggles of day-to-day life. His prose reads like a lonely man's guide to madness where epiphanies pass like gas, and boredom develops its owns manic design. While he writes at angles (and around corners), there is lots of heart behind the lively prose. Most of the characters are wildly off-center, but in the end, you really feel for them. As the main player, Murphy is a clusterfuck of idleness, contradiction, and callousness -- he mistreats women, believes in all things insubstantial - but his transformation from layabout to caretaker (in an asylum, no less) is captivating. The supporting cast is just as entertaining - Cooper, the drunk - Miss Couninan, the embodiment of the lustful Irish - Celia, the sound and caring lover of Murhpy - Ticklepenny, the failed, miserable poet - Miss Corridge, the miserly landlady with a peculiar body odor ('when body odor and volubility meet, then there is no remedy'). These characters give life to a book that at its core is about failure - no matter how hard one tries, the end is absurd, the climax not ascending but leveling off in a flatline tremor. The ending of the book typifies this, consisting of a soaring cruel joke where slapstick turns heartbreaking in one sentence. Beckett throws so much in 170 pages that it's impossible to even provide a synopsis. A high watermark of literature - playful and tragic, profane and manic.<br /><br />Selections from MURPHY:<br /><br />'He had the curious hunted walk, like that of a destitute diabetic in a strange city.'<br /><br />'He thought of the four caged owls in Battersea Park whose joys and sorrows did not begin till dusk.'<br /><br />'Here he was not free, but a mote in the dark of absolute freedom.'<br /><br />'Enter Cooper. Like a mollusc torn from its rock Wylie came away.'<br /><br />'At the corner he paused to admire the pub, superior to any he had ever seen. Suddenly a man was standing in the porch, radiant in his shirt-sleeves and an apron of fine baize, holding fast a bottle of whiskey. His face was as the face of an angel, he stretched out his hand upon Cooper.'<br /><br />'...in the morning nothing remained of the dream but a postmonition of calamity, nothing of the candle but a little coil of tallow.'<br /><br />'...his success with the patients was the signpost pointing to them. It meant that they felt in him what they had been and he in them what he would be.'<br /><br />'...simplicity is as slow as a hearse and as long as a last breakfast.'<br /><br /><br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="281137008352017722" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <a title="framptonhollis" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/59980397-framptonhollis"><img alt="framptonhollis" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1520809126p2/59980397.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="framptonhollis" class="user" itemprop="url" name="framptonhollis" href="/user/show/59980397-framptonhollis">framptonhollis</a>
      </span>

        rated it

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1992995166" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3639416942612305585"><b>The Bridge From Modernism to Postmodernism</b><br/> <br/> "'This is all rather irregular,' said Dr. Killiecrankie.<br/><br/> 'Life is all rather irregular,' said Neary"<br/><br/> I have picked up and put down this novel a few times before, never being able to get through it all in one continuous reading, despite my enjoyment of it and its bizarre prose, absurd comedy, and Worstword Ho! on. But now I have read it all, in full. Tho I will admit to having taken a lengthy break in the middle of my misadventure, I still stand beside </span>
  <span id="freeText3639416942612305585" style="display:none"><b>The Bridge From Modernism to Postmodernism</b><br /> <br /> "'This is all rather irregular,' said Dr. Killiecrankie.<br /><br /> 'Life is all rather irregular,' said Neary"<br /><br /> I have picked up and put down this novel a few times before, never being able to get through it all in one continuous reading, despite my enjoyment of it and its bizarre prose, absurd comedy, and Worstword Ho! on. But now I have read it all, in full. Tho I will admit to having taken a lengthy break in the middle of my misadventure, I still stand beside the fact of my having got through the entirety of this spectacle of absurdity and tragedy and comedy and plenty other words ending in y. <br /><br /> I found this work, in particular, to be pretty difficult, mainly in its earlier sections when Beckett seemed to be bombarding me with his vast vocabulary and literary inventiveness, playfully tossing into the text enough obscure words to make a professor profess to having ejaculated in his flooding trousers because o! how witty and erudite this Beckett chap comes across! Beckett even makes up plenty of terms, terms whose origins are not easily recognizable to begin with, and are often cryptic to an irritating point. But, his prose style is not without its heavy advantages, as this novel simply stuns on a page by page basis with its wit and wonder...and, while it was tough to read at first (and even perhaps here and there but a trifle bothersome and easy prey to accusations of pretention), as the story went on, I fell in love with everything about the text. Every sentence was a treasure to behold, the limits of fiction were stretched into the status of that which is gleefully and excitingly unlimited, and the comedy was on par with some of Beckett's most witty-but not exactly broadly comical-works, and paired itself brilliantly well with the later instances of heavy tragedy that made my bones shake and tremble as I turned the last pages of this masterpiece with reluctant acceptance that this journey was indeed over.</span>
  <a data-text-id="3639416942612305585" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<div id="review_1476160800" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1476160800" />
    <a title="Lee Foust" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/13984676-lee-foust"><img alt="Lee Foust" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1442875445p2/13984676.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1476160800?book_show_action=true">Dec 22, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Lee Foust" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Lee Foust" href="/user/show/13984676-lee-foust">Lee Foust</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780394172101" href="/book/show/1201185.Murphy">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/13984676-lee-foust?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1476160800" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15009593807011699110"><i>Murphy</i> pretty perfectly explicates the literary strategy of the absurd. It's a very, very funny novel--yet also, in the end, based upon the events recounted, it can only be classified as a tragedy. Once it occurred to me that such a contradiction in the classical orders--recounting deaths in a somber or melancholy key and the complications of romance leading to marriage as a series of humorous blunders--is about as good a definition of the literature of the absurd as we are going to get. As fanc</span>
  <span id="freeText15009593807011699110" style="display:none"><i>Murphy</i> pretty perfectly explicates the literary strategy of the absurd. It's a very, very funny novel--yet also, in the end, based upon the events recounted, it can only be classified as a tragedy. Once it occurred to me that such a contradiction in the classical orders--recounting deaths in a somber or melancholy key and the complications of romance leading to marriage as a series of humorous blunders--is about as good a definition of the literature of the absurd as we are going to get. As fanciful and literary as the strategy is, it's the depicting of the ridiculousness of human suffering and our demise, which goes against the grain of literary tradition, that smells lightly of truth. For we know that tragedy can be funny, that slipping on a banana peel is actually quite painful after all, and that sometimes love is a far worse fate than certain more merciful forms of death. Therefore the literary recombination of mood and subject matter reeks of disorder even as it is only a re-combination of already established orders--a tragic tale recounted in a humorous key.<br /><br />Notably, this is both Beckett's first bona fides masterpiece and his first entirely concurrently published novel. I'm currently re-assessing these, my long-time favorite author's works, by reading them in the order that he wrote them and, doing so, it's quite evident how rapidly he improved his writing from <i>Dream of Fair to Middling Women</i> through the story collection and pastiche of <i>More Pricks than Kicks</i> to this very smooth, entertaining, and even, at times, moving read. Finally the narrator frames the tale winningly and lightly rather than bludgeoning us with his ironic and hammy presence, creating many of the novel's entertaining chuckles with his pithy observations and clever turns of phrase. The characters themselves are treated a bit more kindly than those of the above-mentioned first two books and they are allowed to emerge more honestly, it seemed to me, from out of caricature and into portraiture. There's still some artistic distance between characters, story, and the intrusive narrative voice, but I did manage to feel for these characters, despite the cruel absurdity of the indifference of the universe to their petty desires, tribulations, and their eventual ends. Bravo, Mr. Beckett. Now on to <i>Watt</i>.<br /><br />Also it bears mentioning that this novel features, arguably, the greatest first line of a novel ever. But I won't spoil it for you by quoting it here.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="15009593807011699110" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_419052758" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/419052758" />
    <a title="Branko Jovanovski" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/10920656-branko-jovanovski"><img alt="Branko Jovanovski" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1442635591p2/10920656.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/419052758?book_show_action=true">Sep 21, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Branko Jovanovski" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Branko Jovanovski" href="/user/show/10920656-branko-jovanovski">Branko Jovanovski</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer419052758" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13395278560559516864">The sun shone, having no alternative, on the nothing new. Murphy sat out of it, as though he were free, in a mew in West Brompton.”<br/> <br/>For 3 weeks I have been contemplating on writing a review because I was not sure whether a review will do this book justice. And I mean that because no matter how many reviews of this book will ever be written, it will surely have something that one will miss out, something that one will discover in his own ups and downs gained through his personal and social journe</span>
  <span id="freeText13395278560559516864" style="display:none">The sun shone, having no alternative, on the nothing new. Murphy sat out of it, as though he were free, in a mew in West Brompton.”<br /> <br />For 3 weeks I have been contemplating on writing a review because I was not sure whether a review will do this book justice. And I mean that because no matter how many reviews of this book will ever be written, it will surely have something that one will miss out, something that one will discover in his own ups and downs gained through his personal and social journey. But having stared at the closed book for what seems to be a long period after I have seemingly detached from its character, I have decided to write on it. I wouldn’t call it a review, but rather an attempt to understanding what I might have misconceived and discovering what I might have not grasped. Or perhaps there is even no deeper meaning in the dark passages of the story like some might have observed it to be. But what is a better quest of enlightenment, no matter its breadth, than the one that makes us think, the one that challenges our intellect and shakes the core of our innate self-fulfilling prophecy?<br /> As most of Beckett’s writings on the wall, Murphy reeks with elbowing one’s way of purposeless journey. His stoic elaborates of characters and passive and imaginary development tries to reach to the ultimate core of a lost way. He doesn’t even hint there might be the right way; a way at all but lack of purpose as the unconscious pursuit of all these faceless people that rush to reach the light, a light being nothing else but a spasm of the momentary. Whether it is Murphy Himself or any other of his pitiful entourage, the purpose is void in all directions. It is very often to distinguish between the characters of the story. They all look alike and they all seem to have the same meaningless self, joined into a single objective of obstruction. They are all the “nothing new”, that tale of freedom that is transferred from generation to generation to be ultimately lost in its quest within a whirlpool of thoughts and dubious aspirations. No wonder why he tries to depict Murphy as having a split personality, “split self”, trying to distinguish between the imaginary and the less meaningful. Every character from Murphy’s groupie couldn’t care less about him, yet they anticipate, if not cherish his every move. Even his lover, Celia, who is gently depicted as a whore, finds in himself an escape from her situation. She pushes him to be someone who he is not only so that she fulfills her selfish desires of getting away of her dead-end street, without acknowledging any of his reflections and needs. In the end, she fulfills her dream of Murphy finding a job to get her off the street. In the end, he finds the peace that he had never sought of looking for – he finds sanity in the sanatorium. He finds madmen that makes all seems sane. He finds nothing in reality, but even that nothing makes him find a purpose that has never existed, a purpose that never started to exist.<br /> Beckett sets aside a whole chapter to describe Murphy’s “mind,” which “pictured itself as a large hollow sphere, hermetically closed to the universe without.” The constant battle between body and mind he struggles with is perhaps the constant battle of finding that balance that could exist in the ideal world in which everyone prays to; a world in which no one believes in if there is not for that fake comfort of someone else they don’t believe in either. For the first time in the novel, I have felt that Murphy found happiness once his ashes were spilt all over the vomit and the stale beer of the pub he had never intended to spread his light upon.<br /> Irrespective of his message, irrespective of the (mis)interpretation of his writing, the twist of words that his writing entails brings the reader in a superfluous state which could be either a confirmation of the struggle within, or perhaps a testimony of the fear that makes us what we are not.<br /> <br />“The sun shone, having no alternative, on the nothing new. Murphy sat out of it, as though he were free, in a mew in West Brompton.” I could have stopped reading at this point, and I would have still felt this was the novel that had educated me the most. For how many times have we felt out of it, as if we were free, and how many more times will be try to comfort ourselves in a beautiful illusion that might not be real?</span>
  <a data-text-id="13395278560559516864" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_133800965" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/133800965" />
    <a title="Joselito Honestly and Brilliantly" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2190636-joselito-honestly-and-brilliantly"><img alt="Joselito Honestly and Brilliantly" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1349701607p2/2190636.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/133800965?book_show_action=true">Dec 06, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Joselito Honestly and Brilliantly" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Joselito Honestly and Brilliantly" href="/user/show/2190636-joselito-honestly-and-brilliantly">Joselito Honestly and Brilliantly</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer133800965" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer70375976923748652">Even the chess game here is surreal. Its opening called "Zweispringerspott" is--by its sequence of moves and its name--unheard of in the annals of chess literature. Its supposed English translation, "Endon's Affence," does not make anything clearer. Although there may have been plenty of games where players attack and defend both at the same time (which may be what "affence" suggests: attack and defence), I am not aware of any known word in the chess lingo which combines the concepts of attack a</span>
  <span id="freeText70375976923748652" style="display:none">Even the chess game here is surreal. Its opening called "Zweispringerspott" is--by its sequence of moves and its name--unheard of in the annals of chess literature. Its supposed English translation, "Endon's Affence," does not make anything clearer. Although there may have been plenty of games where players attack and defend both at the same time (which may be what "affence" suggests: attack and defence), I am not aware of any known word in the chess lingo which combines the concepts of attack and defense in a single word. "Affence" does not appear in the authoritative and encyclopedic "The Oxford Companion to Chess," for example. Neither does the game between the principal protagonist Murphy (with the White pieces) and the mental patient Endon appear in any of the current chess databases containing possibly all of the known games believed to have been played in this more than 2,000-year-old human invention. Its annotation, too, is brilliantly inventive. White's 38th move, for example, elicited this commentary:<br /><br />"No words can express the torment of mind that goaded White to this abject offensive."<br /><br />This novel is, for me, principally a demented display of the author's writing prowess. Like he's saying that one can go beyond the limits of the English language and still tell an entertaining story. </span>
  <a data-text-id="70375976923748652" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>


      <div class="reviewFooter uitext buttons">
