<span id="freeTextContainer11975729251516030619">I've been a fan of Beckett's plays since I was a punk-ass 18 year old lit student. The dismal, oppressive air, the bleak landscapes, the brooding, the weird buffoonery, his place in letters is well-deserved.<br/><br/>And all that is present in Murphy. Hell, the thing opens with him tied down to a rocking chair. But it doesn't really translate that well to the novel form. I feel like Murphy just would have worked better as a play. Character development? Not really. Narrative? Barely. But the vibe that rema</span>
  <span id="freeText11975729251516030619" style="display:none">I've been a fan of Beckett's plays since I was a punk-ass 18 year old lit student. The dismal, oppressive air, the bleak landscapes, the brooding, the weird buffoonery, his place in letters is well-deserved.<br /><br />And all that is present in Murphy. Hell, the thing opens with him tied down to a rocking chair. But it doesn't really translate that well to the novel form. I feel like Murphy just would have worked better as a play. Character development? Not really. Narrative? Barely. But the vibe that remains, that Beckett is trying to project, is so theatrical that I felt like it would translate well to the stage. The whole thing is just Beckett-images and a parade of grotesque clowns. Keep in mind these are two things I like. It was just a little too much.</span>
  <a data-text-id="11975729251516030619" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

And every final scene from the b</span>
  <span id="freeText18070995759524925761" style="display:none">Re-read this novel after decades and after reading most everything else by Beckett, I better appreciate how this novel anticipates many of the themes and stylistic elements that Beckett will later pursue. I still think the novel is a bit crowded with some just barely conceptualized characters drawn in the rough, and there is more of a picaresque plot to this novel that sometimes feel heavy-handed; but the sentences and vocabulary choices are absolutely sumptuous. And every final scene from the blow-by-blow chess game and after is exquisite. </span>

<div id="review_2003495014" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2003495014" />
    <a title="Simon Robs" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/50133154-simon-robs"><img alt="Simon Robs" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1479583229p2/50133154.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2003495014?book_show_action=true">Jun 07, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Simon Robs" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Simon Robs" href="/user/show/50133154-simon-robs">Simon Robs</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

<span id="freeTextContainer180315220857445227">Funny/sad/funny too absurd for tragic is as Beckett does or will do as he stokes his burner with this debut novel. In "Murphy" his style is more typical to detail narrative than his later work that gets more and more esoteric. Everything that comes later is synecdoche'd here or so it seems. Murphy is the everyman of nowhere doing nothing and wanting out.</span>

<div id="review_49925513" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/49925513" />
    <a title="Keleigh" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/237249-keleigh"><img alt="Keleigh" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1221249744p2/237249.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/49925513?book_show_action=true">Mar 20, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Keleigh" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Keleigh" href="/user/show/237249-keleigh">Keleigh</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/237249-keleigh?shelf=schooldaze">schooldaze</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer49925513" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14096965114975796560">Reading Beckett (for the first time) is like watching a highly adroit but mystifying magician. Or seeing a play in another language. Or coming across a street performer performing complicated and dizzying feats—like juggling on a high-wire while simultaneously turning cartwheels and meditating.<br/><br/>	In other words, Beckett, for me, felt like an entertaining yet befuddling spectacle. I love his playfulness with language, his daringness in using completely inaccessible vocabulary and classic/archaic al</span>
  <span id="freeText14096965114975796560" style="display:none">Reading Beckett (for the first time) is like watching a highly adroit but mystifying magician. Or seeing a play in another language. Or coming across a street performer performing complicated and dizzying feats—like juggling on a high-wire while simultaneously turning cartwheels and meditating.<br /><br />	In other words, Beckett, for me, felt like an entertaining yet befuddling spectacle. I love his playfulness with language, his daringness in using completely inaccessible vocabulary and classic/archaic allusions, and the Shakespearean absurdity and seamlessness of his dialogue. He is not a realist, yet his characters never fail to strike a chord of reality. I wasn’t always sure what was going on, and his language lost me nearly every other page, but about halfway through I gave up trying to comprehend every sentence and just went along for the ride. His genius is undeniable, and I have no qualms with “existential pointlessness.” However, I can’t say I ever got fully taken in by his prose, nor did I experience the depth of feeling that other writers can elicit. He’s in his own class; a comedy of consciousness and black irony, a satire whose “point” or “center” is elusive as a runaway kite. I found Beckett worth reading for linguistic inventiveness alone, but I suspect that the deeper forces at work in Murphy will surface with time. Come to think of it, he reminds me vaguely of Gertrude Stein—an inverse “fool” who confounds with his complexity of words, rather than complicating through simplicity as Stein does. Both writers “get under [my:] skin,” as Stein once rightly declared of her own sentences. There’s no one else quite like them, so perhaps by default they’re like each other. They both get that there’s no answer; so why not play with the mind’s questions?<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="14096965114975796560" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_3258087493" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3258087493" />
    <a title="William" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/100961745-william"><img alt="William" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1601796146p2/100961745.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="William" class="user" itemprop="url" name="William" href="/user/show/100961745-william">William</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback " href="/book/show/16143246-murphy">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/100961745-william?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/100961745-william?shelf=1900s-ce">1900s-ce</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/100961745-william?shelf=beckett">beckett</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/100961745-william?shelf=bookshelf">bookshelf</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3258087493" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7202125106803870761">Murphy is a novel that absolutely shines. It is at once surreal, comic, and very sad. Beckett has a style of prose that is consistently witty and entertaining but can flow into longer and more beautiful passages that really move you. The story is bizarre but never necessarily unfocused, and by the end, Murphy had succeeded in engendering a deep empathy in me for the protagonist and his lover. The hell that these characters seem to find themselves is subtly composed of sublime horrors, made both </span>
  <span id="freeText7202125106803870761" style="display:none">Murphy is a novel that absolutely shines. It is at once surreal, comic, and very sad. Beckett has a style of prose that is consistently witty and entertaining but can flow into longer and more beautiful passages that really move you. The story is bizarre but never necessarily unfocused, and by the end, Murphy had succeeded in engendering a deep empathy in me for the protagonist and his lover. The hell that these characters seem to find themselves is subtly composed of sublime horrors, made both more terrible and more human by how relatable the struggles of this world are. Murphy transmits you to a uniquely dismal world that is but a derision and scornful critique of our own. </span>

<div id="review_1949991254" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1949991254" />
    <a title="Don Livesay" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/24584750-don-livesay"><img alt="Don Livesay" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1389026251p2/24584750.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1949991254?book_show_action=true">Mar 23, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Don Livesay" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Don Livesay" href="/user/show/24584750-don-livesay">Don Livesay</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/19430907-murphy">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1949991254" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11389402572291536134">"Murphy," published in 1938, is by Irish poet and playwright Samuel Beckett, a writer I remember from my days as a theater student for his play, "Waiting for Godot." The story depicts a protagonist whose central pursuit in life is never to be caught doing anything of value. The book begins with the title character, who has tied himself naked to a rocking chair and rocks back and forth in the dark, faster and faster, eventually crashing. Escaping reality is what Murphy enjoys, and you wonder how </span>
  <span id="freeText11389402572291536134" style="display:none">"Murphy," published in 1938, is by Irish poet and playwright Samuel Beckett, a writer I remember from my days as a theater student for his play, "Waiting for Godot." The story depicts a protagonist whose central pursuit in life is never to be caught doing anything of value. The book begins with the title character, who has tied himself naked to a rocking chair and rocks back and forth in the dark, faster and faster, eventually crashing. Escaping reality is what Murphy enjoys, and you wonder how anyone so self-absorbed can attract any friends who care for him. The “friends” in this novel are really an eclectic collection of followers. One day they discover that Murphy has truly “escaped,” or is, at least, no longer around. Their search for him, each for separate reasons, drives the plot. Murphy, unknown to most his world, has finally stumbled (or bumbled) into gainful employment in, of all places, a mental hospital where he works as a nurse. What transpires next follows the strange motivations of his friends as they search (or wait) for Murphy to turn up. The final chapter reveals what happens to him, and the story’s end is ludicrous. But if you piece Murphy’s character together, bit by bit, as I did, the reader has at least a chance to find the ending totally believable. Take time to do this. It’s not a bad way to read this book.<br /><br />I recommend this novel, but as to style, Beckett’s economy of words was often too difficult for me to understand at first read, and I found myself wishing the story was less reflective and more moment to moment. <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="11389402572291536134" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('f7JrZmowLjoRWEmk6oVLfA82Q2TrUpU4owGg8Vl1c/WgHmaBGHOtzjyAUJTUoCb4nK7Pgf8jch1tQezk5FnmEg==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('JjaQKBYcU3uyq01DbeF9UdWDf05yQBZHm+DsEnhv6MD5mp3PZF/Qj59zVHNTxBDVRhvzq2Yx8WJVoKAHxUN9Jw==')})
  }

      <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/1433597.Samuel_Beckett">About Samuel Beckett</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>
<a href="/author/show/1433597.Samuel_Beckett"><div class='bookAuthorProfile__photo' style='background-image: url(https://images.gr-assets.com/authors/1522050561p3/1433597.jpg);'></div>

<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer3210387663375647449">Samuel Barclay Beckett was an Irish avant-garde novelist, playwright, theatre director, and poet, who lived in France for most of his adult life. He wrote in both English and French. His work offers a bleak, tragicomic outlook on human nature, often coupled with black comedy and gallows humour.<br/><br/>Beckett is widely regarded as among the most influential writers of the 20th century. Strongly influenced</span>
  <span id="freeText3210387663375647449" style="display:none">Samuel Barclay Beckett was an Irish avant-garde novelist, playwright, theatre director, and poet, who lived in France for most of his adult life. He wrote in both English and French. His work offers a bleak, tragicomic outlook on human nature, often coupled with black comedy and gallows humour.<br /><br />Beckett is widely regarded as among the most influential writers of the 20th century. Strongly influenced by James Joyce, he is considered one of the last modernists. As an inspiration to many later writers, he is also sometimes considered one of the first postmodernists. He is one of the key writers in what Martin Esslin called the "Theatre of the Absurd". His work became increasingly minimalist in his later career.<br /><br />Beckett was awarded the 1969 Nobel Prize in Literature "for his writing, which—in new forms for the novel and drama—in the destitution of modern man acquires its elevation". He was elected Saoi of Aosdána in 1984.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="3210387663375647449" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

</div>
</div>
